' ' It is unbelievable that Enbridge Pipeline proposes to locate the Alberta Clipper Project less
than a mile from Regina. We cannot believe that the Province of Saskatchewan, the City of
Regina and the Municipality of Sherwood, would allow this to happen. One would think that
these people have no respect for anyone, to fence in the Capital City of Saskatchewan and it’s
surrounding deveIOpments and farmers. We are all for progress, but not to the detriment of the
City and the surrounding land owners with their pipelines.

For example, why couldn’t this pipeline be put 20 miles south of the city of Regina,
starting at Buffalo Pound Lake and taking a south easterly direction to the USA? In this route,
if they wanted to be progressive Enbridge could bypass Regina setting a precedence for their
pipeline, and ﬁnd a new path for all these pipelines, through a less populated area. I hope that the
people in power of the Province will use their inﬂuence and authority, to bring the people from
Enbridge to their senses. We await your reply.

Sincerely,

                                                    Letter of Comment
Date: 7/12/2007 9:22:02 PM
                                                      Proceeding Information
Project Name:     Alberta Clipper Expansion Project                          Company Name:     Enbridge Pipelines Inc.
Hearing Order No:     OH-4-2007                                              File Number: OF-Fac-Oil-E101-2007-03 01
                                                        Contact Information
Name: fred bealle                                                            Address: 1- 257 Emond St
Title:                                                                       City: Ottawa
Organization:                                                                Province:   On
Telephone:     (612) 842-3367                                                Postal Code: K1L 7R9
Facsimile:                                                                   Email: spreensflats@gmail.com
Please ensure that your letter of comment includes:
      ·   the nature of your interest in the application
      ·   comments on the application
      ·   any relevant information that will explain or support your comments
For further information, please refer to the Hearing Order on the NEB website at www.neb-one.gc.ca.
                                                                Comments
                                                        Attach additional pages if necessary.
As a Canadian citizen, I believe that Canadian energy security is an important objective, and that we have already
given away too much control over export of precious and irreplaceable fossil fuels.
This application for expansion to a US site in Wisconsin is very unwise. Under NAFTA regulations, the US has equal
rights to energy resources found in Canada. If the pipeline is expanded, it will be physically possible to export more
oil than without the pipeline. In times of dire need, Canadian oil would be legally commandeered for US markets.
In the absence of this pipeline, exports would be much more difficult, and Canada would have greater control over an
essential resource.
Given that North America has probably seen the Hubbert Peak of production, oil is very likely to continue to increase
 dramatically in value. We should not be allowing American firms to develop and export this resource.June 15, 2007

Michae} J Fi $3196? Enhridge Pipelines 1mg .-

Re: Alberta Clipper Project

Mr Fischer;

Be advised that our family does not want Enbridge employees or representatives on our
land. Changing the location of the Alberta Clipper project and starting a new pipeline corridor
less than a mile south of Regina is ridiculous. Pipeline corridors of this magnitude that are long
term in nature should be legislated to be at least 20 miles from all major urban areas. And even if
our land was 20 miles away, your one time easement payment policy is grossly inadequate and
clearly a swindle of the future owner’s property rights. As a share holder in oil and gas stocks I
am not against pipelines, but I am against surrounding major developing cities with international
pipeline corridors. Regina already has this problem, why make it worse? Especially since other

- pipeline companies who also lack foresight may have more projects in the future, and will likely
follow your route.
Please do not trespass on our land. '
Sincerely, AK
Lyle Benton

                                                                 Comments
                                                         Attach additional pages if necessary.
We own the S half-11-41-7-W4 through which Alberta Clipper will pass.
It is our understanding:
1) that Enbridge is required to bury the pipeline at least 3 feet deep on Canadian land;
2) that Enbridge's plan for reclamation is to abandon the pipe (after some cleaning/scouring actions) where it lays;
3) that Enbridge is required to bury the pipe at least 5 feet deep in the United States.
Given that frost often penetrates more than 3 feet deep, even in areas where there is no surface traffic in the winter,
and knowing that freezing/thawing action and frost heaving does occur, we have concerns that abandoned lines will
 eventually rise close enough to the surface to interfere with normal farming activities.
We request that Enbridge bury the Alberta Clipper line, and any future lines to below the frost line zone. It should be
at least 5 feet deep, and deeper where there is winter traffic over buried lines.
Thank you for your careful consideration and action on his issue.
Keith and Terry Lee Degenhardt

** FILED ON-LINE **
Re: Letter of Comment
    Application for Enbridge Alberta Clipper
   Expansion Project
   Hearing Order OH-4-2007 and
   File OF-Fac-Oil-E101-2007-03 01
Thank you for the opportunity to comment on this application. The Meewasin Valley Authority is a conservation
agency responsible for about 60 km of river valley in and around Saskatoon on the South Saskatchewan River,
pursuant to The Meewasin Valley Authority Act (SK). We also manage a stewardship organization, the Partners FOR
the Saskatchewan River Basin, which is interested in the river basin from the Rocky Mountains to Hudson Bay. As such
we are interested in any proposals that may pose risks to the quality of the water in the basin, both up and
downstream of Saskatoon. In the particular case of the Alberta Clipper Expansion Project, our interest relates to the
river crossing of the South Saskatchewan River. A second interest relates to conservation of natural grasslands in the
Saskatchewan River basin.
We understand that Enbridge Pipelines Inc. proposes to install the new pipeline within an existing pipeline corridor
some 107 river kilometres upstream of Saskatoon. Horizontal directional drilling (HDD) would be used to achieve the
river crossing. The proposed design calls for increased pipe thicknesses in the river crossing and installation of
shut-off valves on both sides of the river. While the risks posed by a new pipeline constructed and maintained to
today’s standards is admittedly small, the consequences of a failure during construction or after the pipeline is in
operation for the riparian and riverine ecosystems of the South Saskatchewan River and to the city of Saskatoon are
considerable. For example, Saskatoon has only about six to eight hours of water supply in its system, should its
supply become contaminated. The effects of a shut down of the Saskatoon water system could be disastrous.
Our questions and comments are as follows:
Crossing design – we wish to be kept informed of the final crossing design and construction plans. This is particularly
important if HDD proves not to be feasible and cut and cover techniques must be used. Although either method of
achieving the crossing can be considered low risk, it is assumed that HDD is superior. We request that any feasibility
studies that have a bearing on the safety or security of the pipeline crossing be brought to our attention. This would
include geotechnical and hydrological investigations. We are also concerned that practices during the actual
construction of the pipeline do not increase risk to the river and its channel.
Pipeline route – we wish to be kept informed of measures aimed at conserving natural grasslands in the
Saskatchewan River basin. Our interest lies not only in rare and endangered species but in grassland ecosystems in
their entirety.
Emergency response/Notification—we notice the emergency staff appear to be in Kerrobert, about two hours from the
river crossing. Assuming that the pipeline’s SCADA system will initiate a system shut-down down in the event of a
rupture, just how sensitive is the system to leak detection? Exactly how are emergency measures coordinated with the
local authorities downstream? Does Enbridge carry sufficient liability insurance to cover a complete shut-down of the
city of Saskatoon’s water system for a prolonged period? Does the method of crossing affect the insurance coverage?
The Meewasin Valley Authority maintains river access sites upstream of Saskatoon at Paradise Beach, Poplar Bluffs,
and the Fred Heal canoe launch site. If details on these sites would assist emergency response planning, we would
be pleased to provide them.
Abandonment—What is the provision for abandonment and can removal of the pipeline at river crossings be
required? Enbridge should establish a fund to pay reclamation, the restoration of natural and agricultural lands, and
other abandonment costs.
Yours sincerely,
Susan Lamb
CEO
                                                                                   Saskatchewan
3211 Albert Street, Room 486                                                                     31 August 2007
Regina, Saskatchewan
S4S 5W6                                                                                          File: 2006 – 147
M. L. Mantha                                                                              FAX: 403-292-5503
Secretary, National Energy Board
444 Seventh Avenue S.W.
Calgary, AB       T2P 0X8
Dear Mr. Mantha:
                                   Enbridge Alberta Clipper Pipeline Project
                                        (Hearing Order No. OH-4-2007)
This Letter of Comment is submitted regarding Enbridge Pipelines Inc.’s proposed Alberta Clipper
Expansion Project (Hearing Order No. OH-4-2007). We appreciate the opportunity to comment.
The comments which follow are based on review of the Environmental and Socio-Economic Assessment
(April 2007) (“the ESA”) presented by Enbridge Pipelines Inc. (“Enbridge,” “the proponent”) in support of
their Alberta Clipper Project (“the Project”). The comments, which are limited to the Saskatchewan portion
of the Project, are based on review of the ESA by Saskatchewan Environment (SE), the principal
environmental-protection agency in Saskatchewan, and the Saskatchewan Watershed Authority (SWA), the
principal water-allocation and watershed-management agency in the province.
As a general comment, we found the information provided, including planned mitigation, to be
comprehensive and detailed. The principal concern in relation to the documentation pertained to the lack of
current site-specific survey data for key concerns (e.g., rare species) and detailed design information for
stream crossings. Enbridge has committed to undertaking a variety of field studies in spring and summer
2007 to address these deficiencies. Provincial agencies will be in a better position to comment on a number
of specific issues and sensitivities once this more detailed information in available.
 1.     Within Saskatchewan, the Project will be located within the watersheds of the North Saskatchewan,
        South Saskatchewan, Qu’Appelle and Souris rivers. It will cross numerous streams, wetlands and
        other features where water is a dominant characteristic: South Saskatchewan and Qu’Appelle rivers;
        Eagle (twice), MacDonald, Iskwao, High Hill, Cottonwood, Wascana, Kronau (twice) Manybone,
        Vipond, Montgomery, Little Pipestone, Jackson and Stony creeks; Chapleau Lakes; 31 unnamed
        creeks and drainages, 10 irrigation canals, 2 drainage ditches and numerous (>200 wetlands).
        The proponent and his consultants engaged in numerous discussions with the SWA (SEA Volume II,
        Table 3.5). The proponent is advised to contact the SWA’s regional offices prior to construction
        within each of the affected watersheds. SWA regional office managers may be contacted as follows:
                  North Battleford (North Saskatchewan River watershed): Don Dill, 306-446-7451;
                  Swift Current (South Saskatchewan River watershed): Rob Wiebe, 306-778-8263;
                  Yorkton (Qu’Appelle River watershed): Jim Waggoner, 306-786-1494; and
                  Weyburn (Souris River watershed): Clinton Molde, 306-848-2347.
        The SWA contact with respect to any use or diversion of ground water in any part of the province is
        Cas Rogal in the Authority’s Moose Jaw office, phone 306-694-3149.
 Michael L. Mantha
August 31, 2007
Page 2
2.      As noted, the Project will cross numerous rivers, creeks and wetlands. Information on these
        crossings and Enbridge’s planned crossing methods in the ESA is preliminary. The ESA does not
        present either detailed information on the crossing sites or, in many cases, the specific proposed
        manner of crossing. The concern is greatest for the crossings of the largest streams (South
        Saskatchewan and Qu’Appelle rivers; Eagle and Wascana creeks). The ESA indicates (Vol. II,
        Section 9.16) that Enbridge will undertake additional studies at the South Saskatchewan River
        crossing and prepare a detailed construction plan for that crossing. Without detailed crossing plans,
        however, it is not possible for provincial agencies to provide substantive comment on that and each
        of the other planned crossings.
        The following agencies will require additional information and consultation regarding the proposed
        stream and wetland crossings:
        i)     SWA’s contact with respect to the proposed crossings of the South Saskatchewan and
               Qu’Appelle rivers and Wascana and Souris creeks is Bart Ogema, Engineer Specialist,
               Hydrology Services (Moose Jaw), 306-694-3957.
        ii)    All of the crossings will need to be reviewed by SE to determine if Aquatic Habitat Protection
               Permits will be required. More specific information will be required for these reviews. SE’s
               contacts for these reviews / permits are as follows:
                     west of and including R. 17, W2M – Maureen Sigmeth, Swift Current, 306-778-8261;
                     and
                     east of R. 17, W2M – Glen Metivier, Saskatoon, 306-933-5026.
3.      Appendix 2A, Figure D-4 indicates that the proposed pipeline may be as close as 2.4 m to an existing
        pipeline in some cases. Kilometre Post (KP) stationing on the diagrams suggests that this may occur
        at the Eagle Creek (KP 392.9 and 424.5), Iskwao Creek (KP 586.3), Jackson Creek (KP 932.1) and
        Stony Creek (KP 942.2) crossings. Enbridge is requested to provide the actual pipeline spacing at
        ALL water crossings to the appropriate SWA regional office (point 1, above). Additionally, an as-
        constructed plan of the pipeline including pipeline marker locations for each navigable river, creek or
        lake crossing should be provided (the South Saskatchewan and Qu’Appelle rivers, Eagle and
        Wascana creeks and Chapleau Lakes are considered navigable). (The ESA indicates that
        geotechnical assessments, water-crossing feasibility assessments, detailed water-crossing
        construction plans, environmental as-built reports and post-construction monitoring have been
        undertaken on existing South Saskatchewan and Qu’Appelle river crossings.)
        This requirements to provide the above-noted pipeline and as-constructed plans to SWA may also
        apply to authorized water projects crossed by the proposed pipeline.
4.      The ESA states that there are no historical records of flooding along the proposed route (Vol. II, page
        5-13). However, the pipeline crosses the Qu’Appelle River which has flooded near the crossing
        point several times in the past 35 years. You should discuss flood levels and concerns with SWA’s
        Yorkton office.
5.      SWA licences surface and groundwater projects in the province. Since not all projects are
        necessarily licenced (e.g., dugouts, farm water wells), SWA recommends that all landowners /
        occupants within 2 km of the Project be contacted to ensure that any unlicenced water projects are
        identified and any owner concerns identified.
 Michael L. Mantha
August 31, 2007
Page 3
6.      SWA regulates surface drainage in the province. Since existing drainage patterns will be maintained
        by the Project, and any alterations will be covered by licencing for the proposed tank farm, SWA
        approval for drainage changes will not be required.
7.      The preferred pipeline corridor re-route (Option 3B) at the City of Regina will cross the Rowatt
        Flood Control Project which is owned and operated by the Sherwood Conservation Area Authority
        (CAA). Approval of the CAA will be required to cross this project area. Contact Larry Wingert,
        Secretary-Treasurer, Sherwood CAA, RR 3, Site 1, Box 36, Regina, SK S4P 2Z3 (phone 306-779-
        0055) in this regard.
8.      Water use and discharge (hydrostatic testing) require approval from provincial agencies.
        i)       Industrial water use requires prior approval from SWA. The necessary approvals may be
                 obtained from the SWA contacts listed in point 1), above.
        ii)      Discharge of waste water including hydrostatic test water may require approval under The
                 Environmental Management and Protection Act from SE. Contact Wes Kotyk, Industrial,
                 Potash, Coal and Spills Unit, Saskatoon, 306-933-6542.
9.      The Project crosses Fish and Wildlife Development Fund Lands (Vol. II, page 5-128) at NW 35-24-
        2 W3M. A Miscellaneous Use Permit will be required. These permits (together with conditions) are
        issued by the SE regional office (Maureen Sigmeth, Swift Current, 306-778-8261). The agreement is
        prepared and fee assessed (based on right-of-way width) by SE’s Land Registry Unit (contact Patty
        Sundet, Regina 306-787-8451).
10.     The ESA refers (Vol. II, Section 2.4.1) to clearing and states (page 2-6) that “non-salvageable debris
        will be burned unless required for chipping or rollback.” While it is unlikely that significant woody
        vegetation will be encountered in southwestern Saskatchewan in particular, given the nature of the
        region crossed and the fact that the line is to be constructed adjacent to an existing ROW, woody
        vegetation may be encountered at some stream or valley crossings. SE strongly discourages burning
        of woody debris in such circumstances. This material should be chipped or otherwise retained for
        application for erosion-control and revegetation purposes. The appropriate SE office (and
        Saskatchewan Agriculture and Food office on Crown land) should b advised prior to any proposed
        burning.
11.     The ESA indicates that the “Construction Footprint” (ROW width) will be 40 m, comprised of up to
        26 m of new ROW and up to 24.3 m of temporary work space (Vol. II, Table 2.1). Drawings in Vol.
        II, Appendix 2A are based on a 40-m ROW width. Other drawings in this section indicate that a
        wider total ROW will be affected when the new line is adjacent to either an existing Enbridge or a
        “foreign” pipeline – total ROW width may be up to 56 m (60 yards, 180 feet). Cumulative ROW
        width is of concern particularly where sensitive areas are crossed. While the ESA indicates that
        efforts will be made to minimize width in sensitive areas, no specifics are provided, and local
        sensitivities should be considered when final design decisions are made.
 Michael L. Mantha
August 31, 2007
Page 4
12.     The ESA indicates that habitat exists for numerous plant and animal species of concern, including
        those listed under The Species at Risk Act (Canada) and The Wildlife Regulations (Saskatchewan) as
        well as species otherwise tracked by provincial authorities. These species have potential to occur in
        areas to be crossed by the Project, and the ESA cites several studies undertaken in support of
        previous Enbridge projects involving lands adjacent to the current ROW. However, at the time the
        ESA was prepared, site-specific surveys of lands to be affected by the Project had not been done.
        We note also that stream and wetland crossings frequently affect areas of remnant native vegetation
        with the highest potential for species of concern. The absence of detail regarding crossing locations
        and methods precluded evaluation of these sites for species of concern.
        While the ESA did not adequately address this issue, the proponent committed to a variety of
        biological surveys (fish, wildlife, vegetation), including surveys for species of concern, to be
        undertaken during spring and summer 2007. Results of these surveys should be provided to this
        office and SE regional offices prior to initiation of any construction. Any conflicts with species of
        concern should be identified, together with an evaluation of the significance of the conflict and
        proposed mitigation.
        In relation to surveys for wildlife species of concern, we note the apparent intent (Volume II, pages
        6-79, 9-6) to limit wildlife studies to segments of the route supporting native vegetation or pasture
        lands which are greater than 500 m in length. Limiting the surveys to areas of this length may result
        in many smaller areas of habitat along smaller streams and valleys not being surveyed. These local
        areas should be surveyed as they frequently provide the best habitat available in areas which have
        been substantively altered by agriculture.
13.     Crossings of Crown land administered by Saskatchewan Agriculture and Food (SAF) will be subject
        to an easement requirement. Additional information (e.g., detailed reclamation plan) may be
        required by that agency as part of their approval process.
        The following SAF regional managers should be contacted:
        i)       west of and including R. 4 W3M - Jim Schmidt, North Battleford, 306-446-7449; and
        ii)      east of R. 4 W3M – Lorne Veitch, Swift Current, 306-778-8300.
14.     Reclamation of all areas of Crown land which support native vegetation must use an approved native
        seed mix. The following additional points apply:
               specifics of the mix must be discussed with and approved by the regional SAF office (see
               previous point);
               all seed for use on Crown land must be subject to an assay by a qualified independent seed lab
               to confirm that the seed mix is weed-seed free. Results of this assay must be provided to the
               regional SAF office prior to seeding;
               subject to landowner approval, SE recommends use of native seed mix when reclaiming areas
               of native vegetation on privately owned land; and
               all equipment should be cleaned before entering areas of native vegetation to minimize
               potential for spread of invasive species (weeds; agronomic grasses).
 Michael L. Mantha
August 31, 2007
Page 5
15.        Pipeline Alternate 3B (preferred route around Regina) is adjacent to the proposed Harbour Landing
           residential and commercial development in southeast Regina. In December 2005 the TransGas
           compressor station near the proposed pipeline experienced a failure and fire. In April 2007, the
           existing Enbridge oil pipeline near Glenavon ruptured and spilled a reported 3800 barrels of oil. The
           need for a comprehensive assessment of catastrophic pipeline failure in this area, including potential
           for contamination of Wascana Creek, should be considered. Reasons for requiring such an
           assessment include the proposed nearby urban expansion, the existence of numerous pipelines in the
           area and recent concern and public criticism regarding the National Building Code (i.e., structure
           susceptibility to external fires) arising from the July 2007 multi-residence fire in Edmonton.
Please contact Graham Mutch of this office (306-787-6241) with any questions regarding the foregoing.

Enbridge Pipelines Inc. (Enbridge)
Alberta Clipper Expansion Project
In accordance with the direction provided by the National Energy Board (the Board) in its
Hearing Order OH—4-2007 of June 28, 2007, Environment Canada is providing this Letter of
Comment to the Board as part of its public hearing into this project proposal. The following
commentary is being provided to the Board in the context of specialist department information
and knowledge pursuant to Section 12 (3) of the Canadian Environmental Assessment Act.
' Environment Canada’s advice is being provided in the centext 0f the follOwin-g Departmental
responsibilities required by legislation and associated policies:
0 Department of the Environment Act
0 Migratory Birds Convention Act and Regulations
0 Species at RiskAcz‘ §
0 Canadian Environmental Protection Act :
o The Federal Policy on Wetland Conservatism ff
(a) In. the preparation of this Letter of Comment Environment Canada has reviewed the 3‘
fellowing documents provided for this assessment: m
o Alberta Clipper Pipeline Prejectg Application by Enbridge Pipelines Ina, Volume II, igég'};
Report on Environmental and Socio-Economic Assessment, April 2007;

0 Alberta Clipper Pipeline Project, Application by Enbridge Pipelines Inc., Volume III,
Report on Environmental and Socie-Economic Assessment, April 2007.
Environment Canada has raised several questions and made several recommendations, outlined
in bold type, in the commentary that follows.
2

COMMENTARY
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
The proposed Alberta Clipper pipeline would extend 1,074 kilometres from Hardisty, Alberta to
the international border at a point near Gretna, Manitoba. The project would also include the
construction of new electrically driven pump units at eight existing pump stations operated by
Enbridge. The new pumps would be installed on land owned by Enbridge and within existing
pump/terminal facilities. New electrical services buildings would be installed at each pump
station. Additional lands would be required for a new pump station to be constructed near
Regina, Saskatchewan. This station would require a plot of land approximately 100 m x 100 on
within a 200 m x 200 m fenced site. The new station would also require a new all weather
access road and electric power supply to the site.
Six new storage tanks would be constructed at Hardisty. Although the tanks would be on lands
owned by Enbridge, there construction would require an expansion on to land currently under
agricultural use. The new land required is approximately 16.8 ha. The tanks would be
constructed within berm containment areas. The bermed tank lots would be lined with
impermeable synthetic liners installed with a bedding of sand and gravel. The bermed area
would be graded to direct all surface water to a runoff containment area, where it would be
monitored for water quality prior to release. Other drainage features would ensure that no offsite
runoff would be allowed to enter the area. Associated facilities required for the Hardisty
Terminal expansion include the installation of three booster pumps, metering and piping.
The new pipeline would parallel the existing Enbridge pipeline corridor for the majority
(approximately 90%) of its length and has four reroutes With approximately 22.5 km of new
pipeline right-oﬁ-way required. The construction footprint of the new pipeline would be installed
in a 40 m wide construction right—oﬁ-way which would include a 2m wide trench.
Pipeline construction would be scheduled for both frozen and non-frozen soil conditions. Clean-
up of pipeline segments scheduled for winter would take place the follOwing spring and early
summer when the land has thawed.
EMERGENCY RESPONSE PROCEDURES
The proponent addresses the subject of accidents or malfunctions at several points in the
documentation, including:
Volume ii, Section. 6.83 subsection 6.2.18 -- Aecidents and Malfunctions, Table 61.6 Signiﬁcance
Evaluation of Adverse Residual Effects ..., Adverse Residual Effects #18 —~ Accidents and
Malfunctions; Appendix 68: 9.0 Spill Contingency Plan, 10.4 Emergency Response; and
Volume II, Section 6.0, Appendix 6D Emergency Contacts, Tables 6D~1, 6D~2, and 6D~3.
In these sections the proponent has provided a general description of Emergency Response
Procedures, including the level of preparedness, safety, response capability and procedures in the

3

event of an emergency. The propenent has provided some detail to understand how the
Emergency Response Programs would work, including the identiﬁcation of emergency
conditions, response procedures and the steps that would be taken to notify or inform all persons
who may be affected.
However, Environment Canada staff believe that there is still a requirement for more
detail in Enbridge’s emergency response procedures. The fellowing narrative is a list of
information needs that Environment Canada recommends. It is our opinion that some but not all
of the information needs have been addressed in the documentation. Environment Canada
recommends that the proponent demonstrate to the Board’s satisfaction that its emergency
response procedures have met the following information needs.
Environment Canada recommendations for Emergency Prevention, Preparedness and
Response Planning for EA Projects: ‘
1. Projeetnrelated Spill Contingency and Emergency Response ?lans shall he provicled to
the appropriate regulatory agencies for review.
2. Plans shall be consistent with industry standard” publications such as Emergency Planning
for Industry (CAN/CSA—Z73 l).
3. Environmental Emergency Response Plans should include:

a. An inventory of petroleum products, chemicals and other hazardous substances
and associated storage facilities and locations to be used during construction and
operational phases. -

b. Identiﬁcation of resources (equipment and staff) to be on~site and/or available to
respond to environmental emergencies. ' .

0. Procedures for responding to spills and releases including an incident reporting
and notiﬁcation system.

d. A list of response organizations and their respective roles.

e. Cleanup and disposal procedures fOr generated wastes.

f. Monitoring and followuup procedures to ensure that mitigative measures are
effective. '

4. Environmental Emergency Response Plans should also address:
» a. The types of emergencies that might reasonably be expected to occur, including
potential on—site and off~site consequences.

b. Prevention (evaluation of’risks), Preparedness (resources & training), Response
(notiﬁcation & mobilization of resources) and Recovery (assessment of damages
and restoration of environment). '

c. Involvement of communities and stakeholders who may be impacted by an
environmental emergency or involved in an emergency response.

Finally, with regard to the information provided in Table 6D --1 of Appendix 6D, Section 6, Vol.
II concerning the contact names and phone numbers given for Environment Canada, the current
information is incorrect. In all cases, i.e. Alberta Emergency Contacts, Saskatchewan

4

Emergency Contacts, and Manitoba Emergency Contacts, the table should be revised to read
Contact: Environment Canada (Environmental Emergencies), Location: Dave Noseworthy
Edmonton, Phone Number: 780-951-875 3.

WILDLIFE

Pipeline Route Alternatives

The proponent states in Section 2.3 that alternative means to carry out the project are discussed
in Section 2.9; however there is 110 Section 2.9 in the report. It thus appears that no alternative
routing options for the pipeline were put forward, only the minor reroutes discussed in Section
4.0 ... Routing and Sitting. From an environmental perspective, the pipeline route crosses several
large tracts of native prairie (e.g. several PFRA pastures, Oak Lake Sandhills) and crosses areas
with high densities of wetlands. EC recommends that the proponent demonstrate to the
Beard’s satisfaction why altemative matings were not evaluated in the deeumehtatien.
With the addition of this pipeline and the Southern Lights pipeline there will be 7 oil pipelines in
the right of way. Environment Canada believes the impacts 0f adding an additional two
pipelines to this right of way in sensitive environmental areas, such as wetland complexes
and wind credible areas should be given further consideration. In particular, the Board
may want to provide its thoughts on what might constitute a threshold in terms of future
additions to the pipeline right of way.

Note that for the Board’s information a list of the sensitive sand dune areas can be found on page
6-7 in “Revegetation of Wind Erodible Soils”.

Species at Risk

No breeding/growing season surveys were undertaken for wildlife and plant species at risk. As
such Environment Canada is unable to comment on potential effects on Species at Risk act
(SARA) listed species. The proponent states that such surveys are scheduled for 2007.
Environment Canada recommends that rare plant and wildlife survey results (and
associated. site speciﬁc mitigation measures) be submitted to government agencies for
review prior to pipeline approval.

Section 6, Table 6.2 page 6-77-78 (9.1a General) the proponent states, here and elsewhere
throughout this table, that additional Wildlife studies in the spring and summer 2007: on. native
vegetation: and pasture land's greater than 500m in length, will be undertaken to identify site
specific features associated with sensitive Wiidiife species. Environment Canada notes that
sensitive wildlife and plant species may occur on smaller parcels. Environment Canada
recommends that the proponent discuss its rationale for not including these smaller areas
in the surveys undertaken: in 2007.

5

Environment Canada further notes that should Species at Risk be affected by the proj ect there is
a requirement pursuant to Section 79 (1 and 2) 0f SARA to notify the competent Minister, and to
take measures to minimize and monitor those effects.
' Mitigation

Re-vegetation of wind erodible soils:

Straw crimping is useful in stabilization of soils; however, there is always the potential for weed
introduction. Environment Canada recommends the proponent explore the possibility of
acquiring native hay and using that for crimping on native grassland and especially the native
sand dune areas.

Detailed mitigation:

The detailed mitigation measures are set out in Table 6.2 entitled “Potential Biophysical and
Socio—economie effects, Mitigative Measures and Residual Effects. . .“. Mitigation measures are
listed under the heading "Recemmendatiens/Mitigative Measares”. As these measares are
listed as “recommendations” and not requirements, Environment Canada recommends the
proponent provide greater certainty by clarifying which 0f these measures the prepenent
Will commit to implementing (e.g. avoid censtruction in PFRA pastures and larger
wetlands between April 1 and July 31).

Horizontal directional drilling appears only to be put forward for road, rail and stream crossings.
Environment Canada suggests the proponent discuss t0 the Board’s satisfaction the
potential for directionally drilling environmentally sensitive areas, such as the sensitive
sand dune areas.

Migratory Birds

Appendix 6A, Detail 6A—5 1a, Wetland Function states that construction activities may proceed
within the nesting period Where areas have been pre—cleared 0r pre~mowed prior to April 1 or if
no nests were observed during nest surveys conducted prior to construction. Environment
Canada cautions that Sites that have been pre—moWed Will still reqtﬁre surveys for nesting birds if
they are located on native prairie as some species may establish nests during the breeding season
and prior to construction activities commencing.

Table 6.2, 9.1(b) page 678 states that where summer construction is required and the area has
not been pre—cleared: the proponent will undertake surveys fer migratory birds on segments of
the route supporting, native grasslands 0r pasture greater than 500111 in length. The Migratory
Birtis Convention Act prehibitiens pretest eggs and nest irrespective of their location_, therefore,
Environment Canada advises that it would be prudent for the preponent to survey any
area that may support nesting migratory birds, irrespective of the iength 0f the site“
WETLANDS
The objective of The Federal Policy on Wetland Conservation is to promote the conservation of
Canada’s wetlands to sustain their ecological and socio-economic functions, now and into the

6

future. The policy’s goals promote: the maintenance of the functions and values derived from
wetlands throughout Canada; enhancement and rehabilitation of wetlands in areas where
continuing loss or degradation of wetlands have reached critical levels; recognition of wetland
functions in resource planning, management and economic decisiommaking with regard to all
federal programs, policies and activities; and utilization of wetlands in a manner that enhances
prospects for their sustained and productive use by future generations.

Numerous wetlands are intersected by this oil pipeline . On page 5-78 (Section 5.1.7 Wetlands)
the proponent acknowledges that wetlands in the vicinity of the proposed pipeline are abundant
and that wetlands intersect approximately 24% (or 260km) 0f the pipeline routing. This is
corroborated by the alignment sheets.

Major wetlands intersected by the project include: the routing at R3364 and R36 south of the
city of Regina, kp883 t0 kp885 near Vandura, kp923.4 near Fairlight, the routing through the
Oak Lakes/Plum Lakes wetland complex to the Oak Lake Sandhills, and the Glenboro/Black
Slough complex As was noted earlier in this commentary, alternative routing options were net
presented in the application documentation. Environment Canada recommends the
proponent provide its rationale for not providing alternative routings that would avoid
these major wetland complexes. Further, that the proponent demonstrate to the Board’s
satisfaction that the proposed mitigation measures Will protect these wetlands from
pipeline failures.

The proponent states that impacts to wetland function are reversible in the short term and of low
magnitude (Page 6-15 last pan). Environment Canada does not support the proponent’s
assertions that wetland function can be restored within a year and that no additional mitigation is
warranted (Cumulative Effects Assessment -- Wetlands. Section 7.2.7, page 7-24). Many of
these wetlands will be impacted by up to seven pipelines. There will be a loss of wetland
function for a number of years until the habitat recovers and in several instances the habitat and
associated uplands will be permanently modiﬁed. Recovery of wetland habitats is also
dependent upon environmental conditions. For instance, during extreme conditions, such as very
wet or dry years, rehabilitation of wetlands will become protracted. For those wetlands that
cannot be avoided, Environment Canada recommends the proponent discuss measures to
compensate for the interim losses. of wetland function that would be consistent with the
goals of the Federal Policy on Wetland Conservation.

A number of more moderately sized wetlands will also be intersected by the pipeline routing. A
pipeline failure may cause signiﬁcant environmental effects to migratory birds and other wildlife
species. Environment Canada recommends the proponent clarify what measures will be
put into place te minimize the risk. of spiils at the foliowing locations: kp476, kpSSS, hp?" 75,
kp783.3, 78355.3, kp812.5¥-815.5, kp847.’7 and 848.7 near Kipling lake, and the large
depreesian at kg]; 161 {in wet years},

The alignment sheets do not provide sufﬁcient clarity for the setbacks from a number of
wetlands. Environment Canada recommends the proponent clarify whether the following
locations are setback 100m frem the high water mark of the following wetlands at: kp277,
kp297.5’, kp301.3, kp348, kp431.4, kp446 (in wet years is a large wetland), kp561.5 PFRA,
kpR65, and kp759.5.

7

The proponent states that it had difﬁculty in quantifying the amount of wetland area traversed or
proximal to the proposed route. Environment Canada notes that dry wetland basins should be
included in the calculations and that all wetland basins should be delineated at the high water
mark (e.g. 1 in 50 year ﬂood line, readily Visible from air photos) irrespective of whether the
basins are wet 01“ dry.

close of the oral portion of the hearing, the NEB will release a draft screening report for
comment. Environment Canada requests. receipt of the draft screening report once it is
available.

The proponent has provided an analysis of the effects on navigation, within the assessment of
environmental effects. Transport Canada’s position is that the effecfs 9f a project on navigation
(i.e., people’s right 1:0 navigate and people’s safety while navigating) should only be considered
in an environmental assessment when the pro} act causes an envimmnemal effect, which in turn,
have an impact on nandgation (indirect effect). Any impact on navigation that: arises as a direct
result of the project shculd not be ccnsidered to be an environmental effect. Direct impacts of the
proj act on navigation are addressed through the Navigable Waters Protection Act and associated
approvals and cmmot be considared to be environmental effects as deﬁned by the Canadian
Environmental Assassment Act (GEM). However, any impact on navigation that arises
indirectly, as a result of an envirmmwntal effect must be considered to be an enviromncntal effect
under CEAA.

Attention: Secretary of the Board:

Re: Letter of Comment, Alberta Clipper Project.

My family is not against the need for pipelines or the granting of easements.
What we are against is short term planning and the inadequate compensation for our
private property. My father worked for a pipeline company and my uncle was a
senior land man. My grandfather owned a fuel delivery trucking service to help pay
the farm. expenses. We are not anti~business tree buggers, but we do believe in
accountability from public and private organizations. The National. Energy Board
(NEB) allows the pipeline industry a monopoly over the land and what is most
disturbing is that landowner’s property rights and fair compensation are in
abeyance. It’s no wonder Enbridge is one of ﬁfty best corporate citizens in Canada
and one of ﬁve Canadian companies ranked in the global 100 most sustainable
corporations in the world.

Why is the Alberta Clipper Project (ACP) proposed to be constructed near
the south Regina city limits creating an all new pipeline alley? We have been led to
believe that this change in the proj ect route is due to resistance and development
obstacles east and north of Regina. The bigger question. is, what kind of plan is it to
locate ah internationai pipeiine within i mile of a sity? A prime exampie of this lack
of foiesight is the city of Regina showing TransCanada Pipeiines in 1992 to
eonstmet a pipeiine that. is now is heing tieyeiopeé around; for residentiai
deveiopment for as tong as Regina deveiops west (see A). Not to mention the
pipeline company Virtually stole the land at the current farmland market value of the
day. What is unrealized that this sterilized land is a huge cost to the landowner,
developer, city of Regina, other future projects that have to cross, and ultimately to
the taxpayers. Also, what is the cost if ever these urban pipelines have a blow out

. .

like we occasionally see in the news? Establishing a new international pipeline
corridor less than one mile from the city limits is repeating past mistakes by
TransCanada and Enbridge (see A & B). International pipelines cause heavy
ﬁnancial damage to potential. development areas future land values and are never
fairly compensated for. Pipeline companies have come to expect the NEB to secure
the land and the courts to impose present day market valuations. Unfortunately the
outcome has become predictable. The NEB will allow Enbridgels planned route,
and product will be ﬂowing through the pipelines long before the courts force a
compensation settlement on us for trespassing on our land. After all we are an
obstacle to progress and international pipelines located near cities are no big deal to
the public interest. In fact, if the ACP has a south route near Regina the rest of the
pipeline industry will surely site this area as the new corridor for all their projects.

The NEB doesn’t. deal with compensation. values, well it’s time it did,
because they are aiding in the big swindle that has been happening to individual
property owners since the railroads were conceived. There is no question that the
presence of pipelines devalues land in the area of a maj or developing city, land
appraisals and sales reﬂect this fact. The NEB has the jurisdiction and can approve
of an international pipeline route but has no say in the compensation paid to
landowners. HOW can the NEB fully regulate and Consider giving a pipeline
company approval. for a pipeline route near cities without fully understanding the
ﬁnancial damage that is done to the future land values? It could be Viewed that the
NEB helps to compensate Enbridge share holders by saving the cost of an extra
pumping station and pipe by apprOVing the pipeline route through Regina, rather
than signiﬁcantly bypassing the city. Are Enbridge’s interests more important, than
spoiling millions of dollars in private land appreciation by allowing the needless
location of a international pipeline by a developing city? If the NEB has a part in
the expropriation Of land it has the moral. and ethical responsibility t0 enforce proper
campeesatien fer ﬁttnte land veh‘ies.

The evitlenee is clear and deeisiye, every easement, exprepriatiom 0i: forced
sale my ﬁlth grade educated gandparents faced (in our land, the future value was
never paid for by pipeline, power and roadways organizations. These areas will
never have a chance for my family to own the land to its highest best use because
it’s now an. easement. How can I replace this land: the Municipality would not

' allow a small subdivision the size of the easement? It should be noted that the loss

these small, parcels of our land in the public interest adds up to signiﬁcant losses in
our families net worth in current dollars. If the NEB has been around since 1959
this means it has been an accomplice to the loss of value of our families real estate.
We are prepared to show the Minister of Natural Resources conﬁdential evidence to
back this up. As you should know, the land is considered a farmer’s retirement
savings plan and money from the easements was put back into our family fami
How would you like your RRSP frozen at some past market value by some
government agency for the shareholders of Enbridge to proﬁt ﬁom?

It’s hard to believe why anyone would have to be provided with evidence as
to limiting international pipeline next to cities. To be safe, you wouldn’t give a four
year old matches and a pail of gas and tell them to be careful, you just know it’s a
bad idea. I have included a picture of a recent gas blow out next to the proposed
Enbridge route (see C). I can assure you that the personnel at this gas storage
facility are the most safety conscious in the industry, as I have attended their safety
seminars. Even with their attention to safety, a blow out like this radiated heat as
far back as the proposed Alberta Clipper Project easement (see D), I know this
because 1 took the picture that made the front page. How many Enbridge bean
counters have pipeline allies for their backyards or live under power transmission
lines? Show me the wisdom. that locating an international pipeline less than a mile
from, the city of Regina is enough distance from urban development. Surely ﬁnther
is better; there is less impact on a lower population density communities and '
because the land value is mostly agricultural, there is less ﬁnancial harm to
individuals a further distance from development. This is not to say take advantage
of them, just make sure issues like future property value losses are addressed. And
if the NEB truly is looking out for the public’s interest, past easement values should
be reevaluated in Canada.

Another swiedle that. pipeline eemeaniee perpeteate ie the taking at mere land
than. they presently need. her example buffer zones and an easement is taken that
has mom fer 5 Of 5 pipelines that may take decades befbre all. the pipelines are
completed. The landowner is essentially giving up land in the future at a past fair
market value price. Ask someone at Enbridge if they would like to sell me shares at
the value Enbridge was trading in 1992? That was the last time a pipeline was put
on our land and that land is now valuable commercial frontage that the value has
been sterilized.

I am, currently having to write this letter of comment during harvest to meet
the deadline. Enbridge land men know fully well from April through November a
farmers main focus is growing food, not dealing with. Canadian pipeline regulation.
Radio advertisements during seeding bombarded us farmers with adds purporting
Enbridge is our friend. This must be why they scheduled an Open house May 3,
2007 while myself and the locals were seeding. I include the map in the paper (that
was given to me) where I got. my notiﬁcation of the project change and the open
house (see E) The yellow line seems to indicate the pipeline easement is some 4
miles from. Regina near Rowatt. They even named the pumping station “Proposed
Rowatt Pumping Station”. I have also included the proper maps that clear up the
misleading route of their open house notiﬁcation map (see D & F). Regina is
actually less than 1 mile away and Rowatt is almost 3 miles away from the planned
route. The radio advertisements and this last minute change of the ACP from north
Regina to south must be really appreciated by the land owners in the noIth!

Some landowners without development potential welcome pipeline easement
reimbursement, why not treat them right and take the pipeline route through. their
land? Just because some landowners in this area maybe agreeable to Enbridge’s
change in route it doesn’t mean they like it. Given the choice most families with a
future intergenerational interest in. the land would say no. I have four neighbors on
this proposed route that will absolutely oppose another easement this close to
Regina. If it causes individuals harm in the public interest is it OK? If you are the
regulator of the pipeline industry you are also responsible for the harm, including
ﬁnancial if you give the approval to proceed.

I see in the media that other pipeline landowners across Canada are fed up
with inadequate easement, lease and adjacent control zone land compensation.
Abandonment, depth of cover; ground water protection, age and condition of
existing pipelines and: sheddy Iandi stewardship polieies ef the pipeliae .inéastry are
also concerns. At the May Std open house i was told that my ideas to locate new
:pipeiines away item uthah areas ancl pay fair compensation fot only what they need
would lead to “$5.00 per liter gas”. A capacity of 800,000 bpd and a capital. cost of
over 3 billion and Enbtidge can’t afford to bypass the City of Regina! Apparently
the pipeline company feels they can manage their business to maximize proﬁt but
my families land that will contain their pipe is not allowed the same opportunity.

it’s time the pipeline companies change their century old way of treating
landowners. Why is it right that a few property owners are expected or forced to
subsidize the many beneﬁciaries of the oil and gas industry? I am not interested in
being forced to encumber future generations as my grandparents have me with the
kind of mandate that the NEB allows pipeline companies to monopolize 011. If the
NEB can’t. regulate international pipelines away from urban development, then what
purpose or how is the public interest served by it?

The simplest solution is sometimes the best, bypass potential urban.
development areas with international pipelines, so that decades later land owners are
not damaged. The Alberta Clipper Project could be the boards Chance to actually
restore the balance for property owners to feel the system works for the few as well
as for the many.

Sincerely,

Lyle Denton

Regina, SK

Re: Alberta Clipper Project Regina route change

I would like to have this attached to my letter of comment as I believe it to be
relevant. Evidently, Enbridge ignored my June 15th notiﬁcation (copy to NEE) of
no trespassing on our land. Apparently three business days after the deadline for
letters of comment, an Enbridge representative was here asking permission to
survey on. our land. When asked if he knew about the no trespassing notiﬁcation
that I sent Enbridge he apologetically said that he was not told. As it turns out I
know his family and I realize he was only doing his job, and it was not his fault.
The fault lies with the process. Enbridgeis arrogance assumes the NEB will rubber
stamp this project change. This process pits neighbor against neighbor, friend
against. friend and the people who resist against an unaccountable system.
Individuals rights are exprop-riated by pipeline companies that get the government to
bring force to bear against simple land owners. Bypass the city of Regina with this
pipeline and all subsequent pipeline corridors and avoid past mistakes.
Sincerely}

\il a {l
Wf 3g;

Lyle Denton

Re: Alberta Clipper Project

Mr, Fischer;

Be advised that our family does not want Enbridge employees or representatives on our
land. Changing the location of the Alberta Clipper project and starting a new pipeline corridor
less than a mile south of Regina is ridiculous. Pipeline corridors of this magnitude that are long
term in nature should be legislated to be at least 20 miles from all major urban areas. And even if
our land was 20 miles away, your one time easement payment policy is grossly inadequate and
clearly a swindle of the ﬁiture owner’s property rights. As a share holder in oil and gas stocks 1
am not against pipelines, but I am against surrounding major developing cities with international
pipeline corridors. Regina already has this problem, why make it worse? Especially since other
pipeline companies WhO also lack foresight may have more projects in. the future, and will likely
follow your route.

Please do not trespass on our land.

Sincerely,

Lyle Benton

Box 37l84

Regina? SK S48 7K4

oe Mr. Dave Batters, MP.
National Energy Board
Minister of Natural Resources
RM. Sherwood
